  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',
        redirectURI: 'https://www.goodreads.com/apple_users/sign_in_with_apple_web',
        state: 'apple_oauth_state_5a210d48-a12a-4737-bc81-d870c5d3e131'
      });
    }
    
    var initializeSiwa = function() {
      var APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL =  "https://appleid.cdn-apple.com/appleauth/static/jsapi/appleid/1/en_US/appleid.auth.js"
      loadScript(APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL, initAppleId);
    };
    if (typeof AppleID !== "undefined") {
      initAppleId();
    } else {
      initializeSiwa();
    }

<div id='communityQuestionsWithAnswers'>
<div class='communityQuestionAndAnswer'>
<div class='communityQuestion'>
<div class='questionText'>
<a href="/questions/1562572-why-does-the-main-character-use-the-metric">Why does the main character use the metric system to describe everything? It doesn’t seem to make any sense. Every room he walks into is described in meters, TV screens: meters, car speed: kilometers per hour. He has no military, scientific or medical training and the audience is American. When does anyone call out kph and not mph and why would anyone know what speed that was when he did.</a>
</div>
<div class='communityQAFooter questionFooter'>

</li>

</li>

</li>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3059128683?book_show_action=true">Dec 15, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="W" class="user" itemprop="url" name="W" href="/user/show/49365462-w">W</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/49365462-w?shelf=thriller">thriller</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3059128683" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13878618950960802916">This book sat on my shelf for years.Whenever I tried to read it,I was put off by the strident anti-Japan tone.<br/><br/>At one level,this book is a murder mystery,and a thriller.If you just consider these elements,it's a pretty good book.It moves along at a brisk enough pace,it has plenty of dramatic tension.<br/><br/>A young girl is murdered in the American headquarters of a major Japanese corporation,and the way the investigation proceeds,makes for a gripping read.<br/><br/>But the trouble is,it isn't just a murder mystery</span>
  <span id="freeText13878618950960802916" style="display:none">This book sat on my shelf for years.Whenever I tried to read it,I was put off by the strident anti-Japan tone.<br /><br />At one level,this book is a murder mystery,and a thriller.If you just consider these elements,it's a pretty good book.It moves along at a brisk enough pace,it has plenty of dramatic tension.<br /><br />A young girl is murdered in the American headquarters of a major Japanese corporation,and the way the investigation proceeds,makes for a gripping read.<br /><br />But the trouble is,it isn't just a murder mystery or a thriller.It is Michael Crichton's no holds barred attack on Japan.Even for a neutral reader like me,finding this much fault with everything Japanese,felt distasteful.<br /><br />I personally,find much to admire about Japan,but Crichton's only concession to Japan,is a few back handed compliments.<br /><br />It has been a while since the publication of this book.Crichton's worst fears about Japan's economic takeover of America have not come true.For me,this book was such a mixed bag.I liked it as a thriller,but I didn't like its vicious tone.</span>
  <a data-text-id="13878618950960802916" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1976401793" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17715223181526151581">Crichton puts forth a very interesting perspective on the economic relationship between Japan and the USA in this novel. <br/><br/>While informative, I feel that Crichton was at times overly pessimistic. He draws a picture of the Japanese annexing the American economy and Japan itself surpassing the US in every degree of first-world status (including GDP). And while he does raise some compelling points, I’m not sure how well the passage of time has supported his assertions. Crichton seemed fully confident</span>
  <span id="freeText17715223181526151581" style="display:none">Crichton puts forth a very interesting perspective on the economic relationship between Japan and the USA in this novel. <br /><br />While informative, I feel that Crichton was at times overly pessimistic. He draws a picture of the Japanese annexing the American economy and Japan itself surpassing the US in every degree of first-world status (including GDP). And while he does raise some compelling points, I’m not sure how well the passage of time has supported his assertions. Crichton seemed fully confident in 1992 that the Japanese economy will soon and inevitably surpass that of the US. Well, here we are in 2018 and Japan’s GDP is still only a quarter of the United State’s. Also our debt to GDP ratio is around 1.2 while Japan’s is at a monstrous 2.6.<br /><br />Crichton may have been slightly out of bounds with the doom and gloom in this novel. I’ll give it three stars because I enjoyed Crichton’s cogent and thought-provoking argument.</span>

<div id="review_923905009" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/923905009" />
    <a title="Supratim" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/21432277-supratim"><img alt="Supratim" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1560446219p2/21432277.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/923905009?book_show_action=true">Apr 29, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Supratim" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Supratim" href="/user/show/21432277-supratim">Supratim</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780099233015" href="/book/show/847999.Rising_Sun">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/21432277-supratim?shelf=mystery">mystery</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/21432277-supratim?shelf=police-procedural">police-procedural</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer923905009" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2240087382378909823">Though I have read only a couple of Michael Crichton's books, I am fan of his writing. His <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/7670.The_Andromeda_Strain' title='The Andromeda Strain by Michael Crichton' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Andromeda Strain</a> and <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/455373.Sphere' title='Sphere by Michael Crichton' rel='nofollow noopener'>Sphere</a> were highly enjoyable. So, with a lot of expectations I started with this book. <br/><br/>From the blurb, I could gather that it is a murder mystery centered on corporate espionage. The body of a young beautiful woman has been discovered in the forty-fifth floor of the Nakamoto Tower - a mighty Japanese conglomerate, in Los Angeles. This was during a party, attended by celebrities, se</span>
  <span id="freeText2240087382378909823" style="display:none">Though I have read only a couple of Michael Crichton's books, I am fan of his writing. His <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/7670.The_Andromeda_Strain" title="The Andromeda Strain by Michael Crichton" rel="nofollow noopener">The Andromeda Strain</a> and <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/455373.Sphere" title="Sphere by Michael Crichton" rel="nofollow noopener">Sphere</a> were highly enjoyable. So, with a lot of expectations I started with this book. <br /><br />From the blurb, I could gather that it is a murder mystery centered on corporate espionage. The body of a young beautiful woman has been discovered in the forty-fifth floor of the Nakamoto Tower - a mighty Japanese conglomerate, in Los Angeles. This was during a party, attended by celebrities, senators - the usual who’s who. <br /><br />The start of the story was excellent. It starts with two detectives from the Internal Affairs Division interrogating Lt. Pete J. Smith, of the Special Services Division of LAPD, who was involved in and ultimately solved the murder. I thought that something terrible is going to happen and my curiosity was piqued. In his investigation, Smith was helped by Capt. Connor – a man who has extensive knowledge of the Japanese people and some pretty useful Japanese contacts as well.<br /><br />This book is not just a murder mystery written to entertain the readers – the author is actually trying to impress upon the readers, what he thinks is the harsh reality of <b> <i>“Japan’s economic behavior, and America’s inadequate response to it”. </i></b> <br /><br />Throughout the book, the author keeps emphasizing how the Japanese are taking over America as in Japan has launched an “economic conquest” of the country. Mostly through Connor and a few other characters, Crichton strives to show the fundamental difference in mind set between the Japanese and the Americans. Connor, who has spent some years in Japan keeps educating Smith about the Japanese way of doing business - their way of keeping American companies out of the Japanese market while at the same time they are acquiring important tech companies &amp; land in the U.S., they have bought control over many American universities, the Japanese spend millions on lobbyists and politicians, have reporters in their pay roll and even bought influence in the police forces. Anyone who tries to come in their way are branded as racists and the Japanese don’t hesitate to destroy the reputation of the their detractors by setting off their pet journalists on them and in cases might send armed thugs who are of course Japanese. The Japanese motto of <b>“Business is war” </b> is not only mentioned a lot of times but demonstrated throughout the story.<br /><br />It’s not just business, but according to Crichton the Japanese are a racist people who always look down upon other non-Japanese people. Connor says <b> <i>“ In many ways, the Japanese are wonderful people. They are hardworking, intelligent, humorous. They had real integrity. They are also the most racist people on the planet. That’s why they’re always accusing everybody else of racism……” </i></b> <br /><br />Crichton vents his disappointment at the indifference of the American public -- <b> <i> " In no other country in the world would you hear people calmly discussing the fact that their cities and states were sold to foreigners." </i> </b><br /><br />In the afterword, the author again reiterates the <b> <i>“adversarial trade”</i></b>  practices of Japan and reminds the reader that Japan is a competitor to America.<br /><br />Honestly speaking, I do not have any knowledge of Japanese trade practices in America in the 80’s and early 90’s – I would not comment on it. I felt his sermonizing took the thrust out of the mystery story and at times the lessons about cultural differences became tedious. To give the author his due, he did do a lot of research – at least the long list of books in the bibliography gives the impression. I felt that the author should have been subtle about his beliefs. May be Crichton thought that a subtle approach would not be sufficient to get his views across to the masses – I don’t know. I have mixed feelings about the book – the sermonizing was a bit too much but the element of suspense was definitely there.<br /><br />As for recommending this book – I have tried to give an honest review, it’s for you to decide.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="2240087382378909823" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_236631813" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/236631813" />
    <a title="Shannon" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/6773727-shannon"><img alt="Shannon" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1579036502p2/6773727.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/236631813?book_show_action=true">Nov 17, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Shannon" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Shannon" href="/user/show/6773727-shannon">Shannon</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6773727-shannon?shelf=books-reviewed">books-reviewed</a>,

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer236631813" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3330517228519013439">In the 80s the big American fear, especially in California, was that the Japanese businesses were going to take over. Strangely, the fact that the Dutch and British had more holdings than the Japanese never mattered.<br/><br/>That said, Japanese conspiracies were popular and this was one of the better ones, which also allowed us to perceive the Japanese manner of thinking.<br/><br/>BTW, this book was better than the movie. Overally, a very good read but not great.<br/><br/>For those who didn't see the movie a pair of America</span>
  <span id="freeText3330517228519013439" style="display:none">In the 80s the big American fear, especially in California, was that the Japanese businesses were going to take over. Strangely, the fact that the Dutch and British had more holdings than the Japanese never mattered.<br /><br />That said, Japanese conspiracies were popular and this was one of the better ones, which also allowed us to perceive the Japanese manner of thinking.<br /><br />BTW, this book was better than the movie. Overally, a very good read but not great.<br /><br />For those who didn't see the movie a pair of American investigators are called in to investigate a murder connected with Japanese business. One of the investigators is very much "pro Japanese" and the other could care less. It's something of a Historical piece now but back in the 80s I'm sure some people felt the Japanese were going to get their revenge for WWII and buy America out.<br /><br /><b> OVERALL GRADE: B to B plus. </b></span>
  <a data-text-id="3330517228519013439" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_3339846645" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3339846645" />
    <a title="Mike" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/7022024-mike"><img alt="Mike" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1470679918p2/7022024.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3339846645?book_show_action=true">May 26, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Mike" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Mike" href="/user/show/7022024-mike">Mike</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7022024-mike?shelf=alternative-history">alternative-history</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7022024-mike?shelf=mystery">mystery</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7022024-mike?shelf=thriller">thriller</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7022024-mike?shelf=book-club">book-club</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7022024-mike?shelf=politics">politics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7022024-mike?shelf=far-east">far-east</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7022024-mike?shelf=also-a-movie">also-a-movie</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7022024-mike?shelf=reviewed">reviewed</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3339846645" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer18259623940668251049">This was an interesting read right after Tai-Pan given that both books explore the business relationship between an Asian nation and a Western one. In this case, though, the Author came to the story with a very definitive message to get across. Namely that America as a whole (industry, government, society) had to stop generally sucking because Japan was kicking our asses in every way imaginable.<br/><br/>Now that might seem a bit strange from a contemporary reader's perspective, but you need to remember w</span>
  <span id="freeText18259623940668251049" style="display:none">This was an interesting read right after Tai-Pan given that both books explore the business relationship between an Asian nation and a Western one. In this case, though, the Author came to the story with a very definitive message to get across. Namely that America as a whole (industry, government, society) had to stop generally sucking because Japan was kicking our asses in every way imaginable.<br /><br />Now that might seem a bit strange from a contemporary reader's perspective, but you need to remember when this book was written in the late eighties the Japanese were very much an economic powerhouse, growing to be one of the largest economies in the world and far surpassing many western companies in efficiency, technology, and quality. Heck, there was even a pretty popular movie about the Japanese buying an American car company and instituting a bunch of changes to the process that was not well received by the American workers who were portrayed as lazy, inefficient, and generally worse than their Japanese counterparts. <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gung_Ho_(film)" rel="nofollow noopener">Naturally this movie was a comedy</a>.<br /><br />This is all to say that the zeitgeist of America at the time was one which viewed Japan as an ascending, threatening economic power. There was certainly plenty of anti-Japanese sentiment in the US due to the loss of jobs and market share at the hands of Japanese competition. Competition that had some serious government protections in their own home markets that prevented American firms from gaining a foothold. For the 1980's Japan was on top of the world. Heck, they even bought <a href="https://www.forbes.com/sites/antoinegara/2017/07/18/ma-flashback-the-takeover-of-rockefeller-center-capped-a-1980s-frenzy-now-a-new-mania-is-afoot/#17f283726331" rel="nofollow noopener">Rockefeller Center</a>. <br /><br />Then the <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lost_Decade_(Japan)" rel="nofollow noopener">Lost Decade(s)</a> happened and Japan has yet to recover.<br /><br />So if you do decided to give this book a shot be warned it will feel very dated with the sinister Japanese forces lurking in the background, pulling the economic and political strings that seek to thwart the hero detectives. <br /><br />Because as much as Crichton uses this story as a soapbox for his public policy prescriptions (and while he does provide the proverbial receipts in the form of an extensive bibliography of economics papers and reports) there is still a murder mystery to be solved. Said investigation brings the reader through all sorts of facets of alleged Japanese business culture and society as well as the depth of their influence in American society. But, at its heart, it is still an interesting and compelling mystery with an almost Holmes-Watson vibe going on between the two detectives leading the investigation.<br /><br />So if you can wade through some late 80's/early 90's Japanese paranoia (which, if nothing else, is a fascinating look at the American psyche from that time period) you will find a pretty solid story and mystery.</span>
  <a data-text-id="18259623940668251049" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_771114241" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/771114241" />
    <a title="Bad-at-reading" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/21536373-bad-at-reading"><img alt="Bad-at-reading" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1371967104p2/21536373.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/771114241?book_show_action=true">Nov 22, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Bad-at-reading" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Bad-at-reading" href="/user/show/21536373-bad-at-reading">Bad-at-reading</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="did not like it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">did not like it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Mass Market Paperback " href="/book/show/9722308-rising-sun">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/21536373-bad-at-reading?shelf=mystery">mystery</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/21536373-bad-at-reading?shelf=very-bad">very-bad</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer771114241" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17718859662252999773">This novel should've been called <i>I'm Not Racist, But--</i>. Crichton's wise men rant against the Japanese as copiously as his straw men do, and their arguments are functionally identical. By all means, he says, paint them with a broad brush, say they're schemers, insist they spell our doom, but good Lord, don't call them "nips"! That's bigoted!<br/><br/>Other times it seems like Crichton isn't even trying not to be racist. The whodunit that fills the first half of the book concerns a beautiful young American </span>
  <span id="freeText17718859662252999773" style="display:none">This novel should've been called <i>I'm Not Racist, But--</i>. Crichton's wise men rant against the Japanese as copiously as his straw men do, and their arguments are functionally identical. By all means, he says, paint them with a broad brush, say they're schemers, insist they spell our doom, but good Lord, don't call them "nips"! That's bigoted!<br /><br />Other times it seems like Crichton isn't even trying not to be racist. The whodunit that fills the first half of the book concerns a beautiful young American woman found raped and strangled in an LA office building owned by a powerful Japanese corporation. That a character makes a self-aware remark about the obvious symbolism only makes things worse. The ensuing investigation is conducted by a Peabody-and-Sherman sort of duo who rather than going on clues or even hunches, use stereotypes to trail the culprit. This is eventually dropped when said technique nets them actual evidence, but the message is clear: racial profiling will get you everywhere.<br /><br />Crichton's known as a master of the "page turner", but here even the pace and structure are wonky. The first half is good murder mystery stuff, laced with (ostensibly) factual information about Japanese business and now-quaint but once cutting-edge surveillance and security tech. Then that falls away suddenly, and the plot becomes a downright slog of a water-treading procedural with confusing and boring political asides. The final act really picks up again with some great should-have-seen-it-coming twists but only because it resumes the murder-mystery thread.<br /><br />The book's also notably misogynist. One female character is the protagonist's archetypally bitchy ex-wife, a lawyer who's so wrapped up in her career as to be a unfit mother for their daughter. Another woman falsely accuses him of molesting her child. The "strong woman", a part-Japanese computer whiz, plays a considerable role in the story, but mostly exists to vouch for Japan's prejudice against foreigners (Crichton repeatedly calls them "the most racist people on Earth") and to provide the sorriest attempt at sexual tension I've ever read. In the world of Rising Sun, women are either spiteful, subservient, distractingly hot, or dead.<br /><br />Japan, of course, lost interest in buying up America, and it was no thanks to fearmongerers like Crichton. But, and call me a frothing radical if you must, I'm not even sure his worst-case scenario would be all that bad. Japan's economic dominance of America would... allow them to exert a certain degree of hegemony, presumably? So what? America's got identity to spare. And it's not like we didn't have it coming. God forbid, he said sarcastically, we fall victim to environmentally conscious, confoundingly polite oppressors. I'm kidding, kind of. But Even the dirtiest deeds committed by the Japanese of Rising Sun are nothing systemic power isn't guilty of perpetrating against us under a more familiar flag.</span>
  <a data-text-id="17718859662252999773" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2626509473" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2626509473" />
    <a title="Debbie Zapata" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/22110057-debbie-zapata"><img alt="Debbie Zapata" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1451631863p2/22110057.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2626509473?book_show_action=true">Dec 14, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Debbie Zapata" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Debbie Zapata" href="/user/show/22110057-debbie-zapata">Debbie Zapata</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780394589428" href="/book/show/762145.Rising_Sun">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/22110057-debbie-zapata?shelf=dar">dar</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2626509473" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1520144600935844175">After two duds in a row I felt like something snappy and Michael Crichton will certainly deliver snappy.<br/><br/>If I had ever read this book before, it was at least 8 to 10 years ago but I kept thinking how familiar the story was, and after just a few pages it dawned on me that I had watched the movie a gazillion times. As soon as I made that connection (how could I have forgotten?!) I saw the movie in my head as I read the book. Not necessarily a bad thing, but it meant I already knew whodunnit, why th</span>
  <span id="freeText1520144600935844175" style="display:none">After two duds in a row I felt like something snappy and Michael Crichton will certainly deliver snappy.<br /><br />If I had ever read this book before, it was at least 8 to 10 years ago but I kept thinking how familiar the story was, and after just a few pages it dawned on me that I had watched the movie a gazillion times. As soon as I made that connection (how could I have forgotten?!) I saw the movie in my head as I read the book. Not necessarily a bad thing, but it meant I already knew whodunnit, why they dunnit, and what happened after they dunnit, so reading was a little bit more of a simple tidy-up than would have been the case otherwise. <br /><br />I didn't let my advance knowledge keep me from enjoying this fast and furious mystery, though. I actually liked the visions of Sean Connery and Wesley Snipes dancing snappily in my head as they rushed around trying to catch up to the killer. They always seemed to be just a few minutes too late,<br />whichever way they turned. <br /><br />I did wonder if America was ever as scared of the Japanese as the author seemed to feel, and sometimes the fear seemed blown way out of proportion, but I liked the book almost as much as I liked the movie. <br /><br />(And I am only putting it on my dar shelf to give away because I don't think I could manage another reading, Connery and Snipes in my head or not.)<br /><br />One more title to review and I will be caught up with the reading I have done since Nov 13th. Whew!<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="1520144600935844175" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer16613096894310564251">Michael Crichton is a very interesting writer. He takes a subject that he is interested in, researches it fully and then crafts a story around the research.<br/><br/>The blurb for "Rising Sun" describes it as a business thriller. There aren't actually any thrills in it, but it is very interesting, as a description of how Japanese big business is buying corporate America.<br/><br/>I enjoy learning stuff while I'm reading, but if you just wanted a thriller, this might not be for you.</span>

<div id="review_203329784" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/203329784" />
    <a title="Laura Grable" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3776663-laura-grable"><img alt="Laura Grable" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1274729856p2/3776663.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/203329784?book_show_action=true">Aug 29, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Laura Grable" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Laura Grable" href="/user/show/3776663-laura-grable">Laura Grable</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780394589428" href="/book/show/762145.Rising_Sun">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer203329784" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6624311294408133226">I really enjoy how Michael Crichton can keep me glued to the page with plot twists and fast pacing, but I really hate when he gets too preachy. I think that is his biggest weakness as a writer. All of his books have some kind of lesson to be learned, typically it's the dangers of fast-growing technology, but his best novels show the reader why this is a problem instead of insistently telling us. For example, in Jurassic Park we didn't need a lecture on the dangers of playing God and giving life </span>
  <span id="freeText6624311294408133226" style="display:none">I really enjoy how Michael Crichton can keep me glued to the page with plot twists and fast pacing, but I really hate when he gets too preachy. I think that is his biggest weakness as a writer. All of his books have some kind of lesson to be learned, typically it's the dangers of fast-growing technology, but his best novels show the reader why this is a problem instead of insistently telling us. For example, in Jurassic Park we didn't need a lecture on the dangers of playing God and giving life to an extinct species, the dinosaurs pretty much took care of that. So, due to his constant rants about the Japanese taking over America, I must give this book two stars, even though I found the murder mystery to be suspenseful and interesting. <br /><br />The funny thing is that his fears about the American economy aren't entirely unfounded. I think the Japanese taking over must be an out-dated fear, because I have never heard such fears, but he does make good points about our economy going too far into debt and other countries passing us in education and industry. However, his characterizations of the Japanese seem very harsh. No matter how many times the characters insist that they aren't judging the Japanese culture, it sure seems like they are vilifying them. All this distracts from the murder case, which is pretty compelling. Overall, even though I couldn't put this book down because I wanted to know the identity of the murderer, it left a bad taste in my mouth and, as a book by Crichton, comes off as pretty forgettable. </span>
  <a data-text-id="6624311294408133226" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/32410769" />
    <a title="Stephen" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1413439-stephen"><img alt="Stephen" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1311616811p2/1413439.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/32410769?book_show_action=true">Sep 08, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Stephen" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Stephen" href="/user/show/1413439-stephen">Stephen</a>

<div id="review_2050761228" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2050761228" />
    <a title="Kim" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/6143501-kim"><img alt="Kim" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1489623972p2/6143501.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2050761228?book_show_action=true">Jul 05, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Kim" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Kim" href="/user/show/6143501-kim">Kim</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2050761228" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3672154074151066273">This book is now 25 years old, and while it holds up as a murder mystery the politics of it are a bit outdated. The late Michael Crichton wrote the book as the personal computer industry was in flux. Much of the industry was starting to be transferred to Asian markets. In addition, the US was facing increasing trade deficits with Japan, the auto industry was in trouble, almost all televisions were being built there as well.<br/><br/>In the quarter century since then the trade deficit has shifted to China </span>
  <span id="freeText3672154074151066273" style="display:none">This book is now 25 years old, and while it holds up as a murder mystery the politics of it are a bit outdated. The late Michael Crichton wrote the book as the personal computer industry was in flux. Much of the industry was starting to be transferred to Asian markets. In addition, the US was facing increasing trade deficits with Japan, the auto industry was in trouble, almost all televisions were being built there as well.<br /><br />In the quarter century since then the trade deficit has shifted to China and South Korea, US industrialists moved their own factories to Asia for the cheap labor, and once again we're faced with having a major trading partner in which leaders and consumers think in fundamentally different ways than Americans. As for Japan, they went through a major recession from which they still haven't fully recovered after several years.<br /><br />When this was written there was quite a bit of general irritation directed at Japan. Crichton picked up on this irritation and, in more than a few ways, directed it out to the Japanese people themselves while still expressing envy about their culture.<br /><br />The central story of the book is a murder mystery. The book is set in Los Angeles and during the opening of a new Japanese-owned corporate building a young white woman is found dead in a conference room in one of the upper floors. The Japanese managers refuse to allow the investigation to continue until they have a liaison officer (Lt. Peter Smith) who is encouraged to pick up a more experience former officer on the way to the scene named John Connor. Connor has lived in Japan and is fluent in the language. It's through his eyes that the Japanese culture is revealed, both good and bad. Connor frequently expresses envy for the cohesiveness of the Japanese while calling them the most racist nation on earth. Their business foresight is lauded while they're cursed for creating a market closed to American goods while dumping Japanese goods in the American market for cheaper than Tokyo prices.  Meanwhile, note the several times that the two investigators hit potholes as they drive around Los Angeles, the clear implication being that such things would never be allowed in Japan.<br /><br />This split attitude runs through the entire book as the two work together to unravel the mystery while navigating the layers of Japan-born executives of the company that owns the building.<br /><br />The mystery holds up, though much of it revolves around the search for original tapes of the crime and some technology that now seems commonplace. The ongoing lessons from John Connor on Japanese business practices and culture are interesting even if some of the interpretations are over the top.<br /><br />Crichton tends to blame regulation, which would seem to have much less influence than poor trade negotiations, but Crichton was more than a little conservative so it's no surprise where his focus would be. In the years since the book was written the World Trade Organization (WTO) stepped in a few times to sanction Japan for dumping computer chips in the US market, meanwhile US corporations seem to be determined to continue a downward spiral of underpaying workers, overpaying executives, sending manufacturing to Asia, and then wondering why the American consumer can't afford their products. In those ways not much has changed over a quarter century.<br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="3672154074151066273" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

While the story was good, the real value here is a look inside the Japanese culture. Reading is fun, but it's even better when you learn something ab</span>
  <span id="freeText7907454825887744855" style="display:none">Solid read. Good, not great, but better than most. Not my favorite Chrichton book, but better than most I've read. Am I being repetitious? <br /><br />It's a unique detective story. <div id="review_18718023" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/18718023" />
    <a title="Brad" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1022982-brad"><img alt="Brad" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1442484575p2/1022982.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/18718023?book_show_action=true">Mar 26, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Brad" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Brad" href="/user/show/1022982-brad">Brad</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Audio 9780679410997" href="/book/show/452279.Rising_Sun">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1022982-brad?shelf=mystery">mystery</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1022982-brad?shelf=underwhelming">underwhelming</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1022982-brad?shelf=technobabble">technobabble</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1022982-brad?shelf=soap-box">soap-box</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1022982-brad?shelf=audio-book">audio-book</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1022982-brad?shelf=police-procedural">police-procedural</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1022982-brad?shelf=a-floating-bar-of-ivory">a-floating-bar-of-ivory</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer18718023" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2534052738060154498">Listening to Michael Crichton -- at least judging by this one outing -- is very different than reading Michael Crichton. <br/><br/>I know, I know, listening to anything is different than reading, but I was shocked to discover how much lecturing Crichton does in his books. It goes far beyond the usual exposition of tech and ideas one would expect from a Sci-Fi writer. His lectures are long, over-blown, bordering on excruciating, but I think the magic of the written words allows those moments to be glossed </span>
  <span id="freeText2534052738060154498" style="display:none">Listening to Michael Crichton -- at least judging by this one outing -- is very different than reading Michael Crichton. <br /><br />I know, I know, listening to anything is different than reading, but I was shocked to discover how much lecturing Crichton does in his books. It goes far beyond the usual exposition of tech and ideas one would expect from a Sci-Fi writer. His lectures are long, over-blown, bordering on excruciating, but I think the magic of the written words allows those moments to be glossed over. Put voice to them, however, and even with an excellent performance by MacLeod Andrews, they take on a pedantic flavour that sours the characterization of every minor character Smith and Connor come in contact with. <br /><br />And then there is the xenophobia. Crichton's xenophobia towards the Japanese is so pressurized it blows the top off the thermometer and sends mercury spilling everywhere, and it makes some portions of the story nearly unreadable. <br /><br />Even so, the murder mystery is surprisingly compelling, and John Connor, especially, is a character I want to spend as much time with as I can. <i>Rising Sun</i> is far from Crichton's best, and it could be close to his worst, but I still found myself plowing through the audiobook version at top speed. He may not have been the nicest cat or the coolest cat, but he sure coudl spin a yarn -- when he wasn't pontificating.</span>
  <a data-text-id="2534052738060154498" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <a title="Kevin Lake" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1923849-kevin-lake"><img alt="Kevin Lake" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1263189440p2/1923849.jpg" /></a>

      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer391914704" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10454328226530809490">"Rising Sun" is Micheal Crichton at his best. All the reasons he is one of my favorite authors are found in this book. He writes honestly, not politically correctly. The cold hard facts he states, through the eyes and ears and mouths of his characters, about the Japanese and their business practices and America's inability to respond to either, mostly out of ignorance, innefeciency, and a desire to, at whatever cost, not come across as offensive or racist, is spot on. I live in Asia full time (t</span>
  <span id="freeText10454328226530809490" style="display:none">"Rising Sun" is Micheal Crichton at his best. All the reasons he is one of my favorite authors are found in this book. He writes honestly, not politically correctly. The cold hard facts he states, through the eyes and ears and mouths of his characters, about the Japanese and their business practices and America's inability to respond to either, mostly out of ignorance, innefeciency, and a desire to, at whatever cost, not come across as offensive or racist, is spot on. I live in Asia full time (the Philippines) and have been to Japan, and China and South Korea and have experienced racism, as a white man, that is not only accepted in Asian culture, but practiced by the masses, and taught to children openly by parents and educators. Practices and behaviors that, thank God, are not accepted or tolerated in the west, and Crichton is NOT afraid to write about that issue and call a spade a mother lovin' spade. <br />And that style of writing is so refreshing to me.<br />I also enjoy how he wraps fact with fiction in his work, as he does in this one, so not only do you get one hell of an exciting ride with the story line, but you learn so many facts, some of which are not always comfortable (like America represents 4% of the earth's surface population, has 18% of the world economy, yet has 50% of the world's lawyers due to our legal frivolity). <br />This book ranks as a close second as my favorite Crichton work, first being "State of Fear."</span>
  <a data-text-id="10454328226530809490" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_103091258" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/103091258" />
    <a title="Matt" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1645159-matt"><img alt="Matt" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1336316055p2/1645159.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/103091258?book_show_action=true">May 16, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Matt" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Matt" href="/user/show/1645159-matt">Matt</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="did not like it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">did not like it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer103091258" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9550215426467391746">In the early to mid-1990's a wave of anti-Japan hysteria swept through some segments of the American population. I distinctly remember watching two newscasts from this time concerning Japan. One talked about people's fear of Japanese 'interests' buying up significant portions of the U.S. The other showed video clip of people venting their anger over Japanese imports by destroying a Toyota pickup with baseball bats and the reporter (off-screen) explaining that the truck was built in the U.S. at t</span>
  <span id="freeText9550215426467391746" style="display:none">In the early to mid-1990's a wave of anti-Japan hysteria swept through some segments of the American population. I distinctly remember watching two newscasts from this time concerning Japan. One talked about people's fear of Japanese 'interests' buying up significant portions of the U.S. The other showed video clip of people venting their anger over Japanese imports by destroying a Toyota pickup with baseball bats and the reporter (off-screen) explaining that the truck was built in the U.S. at the time. <br /><br />This book was written during this time period and I felt that Crichton was at best exploiting an irrational fear of some people that was also a 'hot topic' at the time. At worst, this book was racist towards Japanese. Not sure which it was, but was not impressed either way. The inclusion of certain sexual practices into the murder mystery did not help either. <br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="9550215426467391746" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_639758844" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/639758844" />
    <a title="William Galaini" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/18174482-william-galaini"><img alt="William Galaini" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1442888413p2/18174482.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/639758844?book_show_action=true">Jun 12, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="William Galaini" class="user" itemprop="url" name="William Galaini" href="/user/show/18174482-william-galaini">William Galaini</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer639758844" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1058854269067938288">Underwhelming and transparent would be the two words I would use for this novel by Crichton.<br/><br/>Starring two completely replaceable and indistinct noir detectives, we find ourselves tugged along an unnecessarily winding plot filled with conveniently entertaining twists and turns and at the center; a sexy femme fatale lies dead without panties.<br/><br/>Here are the tropes that this novel is a slave to:<br/> 1.) A car chase between the police and a sports car that ends in a flaming wreck.<br/> 2.) Dead suspects are not </span>
  <span id="freeText1058854269067938288" style="display:none">Underwhelming and transparent would be the two words I would use for this novel by Crichton.<br /><br />Starring two completely replaceable and indistinct noir detectives, we find ourselves tugged along an unnecessarily winding plot filled with conveniently entertaining twists and turns and at the center; a sexy femme fatale lies dead without panties.<br /><br />Here are the tropes that this novel is a slave to:<br /> 1.) A car chase between the police and a sports car that ends in a flaming wreck.<br /> 2.) Dead suspects are not dead after all!<br /> 3.) Naked women distract the police so their man can get away!<br /> 4.) Foreigners are bad!<br /> 5.) Someone altered the tapes!<br /> 6.) The security team is slowing the investigation because of a cover-up!<br /> 7.) The chief of police is really riding the detectives!<br /> 8.) Oh no! Case closed, but the detectives have a hunch!<br /><br />I could go on and on. This is a by-the-numbers novel that would have been considered edgy in the seventy's but certainly no later than that. Women are either the victims of crimes or the prizes of victory (except for the one who is crippled; she is just outcast) and the men are steely and determined with charming masculine flaws like drinking or failing at relationships. This is low brow, pandering fiction where the characters generate five-page conversations about Japanese/American business relations and culture clashes.<br /><br />Underwhelmed. Unimpressed. Rising Sun is a book built strictly with the intention of delivering a perspective on international business that is tainted with xenophobia. It is bland in its writing and the elements are all things we've seen elsewhere. Give this a pass regardless of the name on the cover.</span>
  <a data-text-id="1058854269067938288" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('cV1qIKGtQQrTiwnCiYsRMngJ74QoRxeaxLudoAxi6tJ/m6Pe3wA8YsyMP6PJInUn1qDMvVXmM6v1Qcpgyp7efg==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

Yet there are moments of exquisite tenderness in these troubled lives. Georgette, the transvestite who falls in love with a callous hoodlum; Tralala, the conniving prostitute who plumbs the depths of sexual degradation; and Harry, the strike leader who hides his true desires behind a boorish masculinity, are unforgettable creations. Last Exit to Brooklyn was banned by British courts in 1967, a decision that was reversed the following year with the help of a number of writers and critics including Anthony Burgess and Frank Kermode.\n\nHubert Selby, Jr. (1928-2004) was born in Brooklyn, New York. At the age of 15, he dropped out of school and went to sea with the merchant marines. While at sea he was diagnosed with lung disease. With no other way to make a living, he decided to try writing: \'I knew the alphabet. Maybe I could be a writer.\' In 1964 he completed his first book, Last Exit to Brooklyn, which has since become a cult classic. In 1966, it was the subject of an obscenity trial in the UK. His other books include The Room, The Demon, Requiem for a Dream, The Willow Tree and Waiting Period. In 2000, Requiem for a Dream was adapted into a film starring Jared Leto and Ellen Burstyn, and directed by Darren Aronofsky.\n\n\'Last Exit to Brooklyn will explode like a rusty hellish bombshell           <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>
<a href="/author/show/5194.Michael_Crichton"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://images.gr-assets.com/authors/1359042651p3/5194.jpg);'></div>

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__widgetContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__name'>
<a href="/author/show/5194.Michael_Crichton">Michael Crichton

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer11221357055368054309">Michael Crichton (1942-2008) was one of the most successful novelists of his generation, admired for his meticulous scientific research and fast-paced narrative. He graduated summa cum laude and earned his MD from Harvard Medical School in 1969. His first novel, <i>
  <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/18078737.Odds_On' title='Odds On' rel='nofollow noopener'>Odds On</a>
</i> (1966), was written under the pseudonym <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/12619.John_Lange' title='John Lange' rel='nofollow noopener'>John Lange</a> and was followed by seven more Lange novels. He also wrote as <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/85503.Michael_Douglas' title='Michael Douglas' rel='nofollow noopener'>Michael Doug</a></span>
  <span id="freeText11221357055368054309" style="display:none">Michael Crichton (1942-2008) was one of the most successful novelists of his generation, admired for his meticulous scientific research and fast-paced narrative. He graduated summa cum laude and earned his MD from Harvard Medical School in 1969. His first novel, <i>
  <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/18078737.Odds_On" title="Odds On" rel="nofollow noopener">Odds On</a>
</i> (1966), was written under the pseudonym <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/12619.John_Lange" title="John Lange" rel="nofollow noopener">John Lange</a> and was followed by seven more Lange novels. He also wrote as <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/85503.Michael_Douglas" title="Michael Douglas" rel="nofollow noopener">Michael Douglas</a> and <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/607284.Jeffery_Hudson" title="Jeffery Hudson" rel="nofollow noopener">Jeffery Hudson</a>. His novel <i>
  <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/7663.A_Case_of_Need" title="A Case of Need" rel="nofollow noopener">A Case of Need</a>
</i> won the Edgar Award in 1969. Popular throughout the world, he has sold more than 200 million books. His novels have been translated into thirty-eight languages, and thirteen have been made into films.<br /><br />Michael Crichton passed away from lymphoma in 2008. He was 66 years old.</span>
  <a data-text-id="11221357055368054309" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>
